Wealthy investors stay in Hong Kong... For nowCE Noticias Financieras EnglishAugust 12, 2020 WednesdayCopyright 2020 Content Engine, LLC.All Rights ReservedCopyright 2020 CE Noticias Financieras All Rights ReservedLength: 540 wordsBody(Bloomberg) -- They have planned exit routes, opened bank accounts in other countries, and secured foreign passports.But, at least for now, most of Hong Kong's high net worth investors stay in the city, alleviating the fear of the city's new national security law causing a wave of capital outflows.Withdrawals have been minimal since the law came into force on June 30, in part because wealthy investors are still assessing how the Chinese government's stronger control of Hong Kong will affect asset prices and the long-term business environment, according to executives from four Asian wealth managers, who called for their identity not to be disclosed because of private information.Detractors of the legislation have argued that it will undermine Hong Kong's role as a financial center, but some wealthy investors are open to an alternative narrative -- supported by the Hong Kong government and the city's wealthiest tycoons -- that the law will help stabilize an economy hit by months of pro-democracy protests. That view has been reinforced by signs of tacit support from Beijing, such as record purchases of Hong Kong stocks by Chinese funds and a series of high-profile IPO outlets by the nation's tech giants."They are likely to be opening up offshore accounts and considering leaving, but the preparations will not lead to a major exodus of capital," said Kenny Wen, Hong Kong's Hong Kong strata Sun Hung Kai Co. "Money is moving towards Hong Kong at the same time because investors are optimistic about the Hong Kong stock market , especially with the new quotes."It is not known whether these optimistic prospects will endure, especially with Hong Kong caught in the crossfire of an increasingly intense confrontation between China and the United States. But the result since June has been a strong Hong Kong currency, a resilient real estate market and stable stock prices. Capital inflows have been so significant in recent weeks that authorities intervened to prevent the local currency from exceeding the strong end of their trading band, far away from the apocalyptic scenario anticipated by Hong Kong bassists like Kyle Bass.Many wealthy investors who have kept their money in the city have cited expectations that the Chinese government will act to shore up Hong Kong's markets, wealth managers said.Bahren Shaari, bank of Singapore's chief executive, said most are "taking a wait-to-see approach.""They have taken steps to make sure they have positioned themselves if the event is worse than expected or better than expected," Shaari said in an interview on Bloomberg Television on Wednesday.The city's equity inflows through the continental's market links increased more than 100 percent from the previous year to US$16.6 billion since early June, fueling speculation that Chinese government funds are buying."We can expect more business opportunities and greater growth margin for Hong Kong's financial industry," said Eddie Yue, executive director of the Hong Kong Monetary Authority last month. "As we all know, a safe and stable social environment is what every investor is looking for and what he's comfortable with."Original Note:Wealthy Hong Kong Investors Stay Put With Crisis Plans Ready (1)©2020 Bloomberg L.P.Load-Date: August 13, 2020End of Document

Microsoft wants to buy TikTok, which could save the app from the US vetoCE Noticias Financieras EnglishAugust 3, 2020 MondayCopyright 2020 Content Engine, LLC.All Rights ReservedCopyright 2020 CE Noticias Financieras All Rights ReservedLength: 552 wordsBodyMicrosoft has confirmed its intention to purchase the TikTok music microvideo platform in the United States after its CEO, Satya Nadella, had a conversation with US President Donald Trump, who had announced its intention to ban Chinese app in the country."Microsoft is ready to continue discussions to explore the purchase of TikTok in the United States,"the company has informed through a statement disseminated through its 'blog'.The Redmond giant will engage in talks with ByteDance, the company that owns TikTok, "quickly" with the aim of completing the negotiations "within weeks". On the possible date, it has progressed that discussions may be complete "by September 15". First Solar Issues Public Comment on DOE NoticeTargeted News ServiceAugust 26, 2020 Wednesday GENERAL_EDIT_CORRECTION ERROR Document ID 8 Has error Validate old format failed - Input Date Invalid - 00:31: Unparseable date: "00:31" ESTCopyright 2020 Targeted News Service LLC All Rights ReservedLength: 4805 wordsByline: Targeted News ServiceDateline: WASHINGTON BodyWASHINGTON, Aug. 26 -- Julie Myerholtz, chief information security officer of First Solar Inc., Toledo, Ohio, has issued a public comment on the Department of Energy notice entitled "Securing the United States Bulk-Power System". The comment was written on Aug. 24, 2020, and posted on Aug. 25, 2020:* * *IntroductionFounded in Ohio in 1999, and headquartered in Arizona, First Solar is America's largest solar manufacturer. It is also the only Solar Module Super League manufacturer to innovate and design its products in the United States (U.S.). First Solar has spent over one billion dollars in research and development across the last decade, and consistently sets a global benchmark for quality and reliability. PVEL, which is the leading independent lab tracking the downstream solar industry, recently recognized First Solar in its PV Module Reliability Scorecard as a Top Performer in every test completed.With over 25 gigawatts of thin-film photovoltaic (PV) modules shipped globally and a culmination of two decades of experience and expertise across the entire utility-scale solar power plant value chain, First Solar has independently weathered storms that have claimed other solar manufacturers while doubling down on its commitment to invest in the U.S. First Solar is a standout leader in an industry otherwise dominated by manufacturers based in China, with over 2,500 jobs in the U.S., including in our Ohio-based manufacturing facilities.Grid security plays an increasingly critical role in economic growth and in the overall security of America's Bulk Power System (BPS). We believe the potential for technological manipulation of energy supply at the power generation level presents a real risk. Any component connected to our nation's grid must be secure and sourced from trusted suppliers that are not affiliated with, controlled by, or manufactured in any adversarial country. Our BPS security is only as strong as the weakest link, and given our extensive experience throughout the utility-scale PV industry, it is First Solar's viewpoint that modules can be controlled remotely. In safeguarding America's electric grid, the U.S. has the opportunity to examine each link in order to protect our energy independence and defend against hidden attacks.For this reason we recommend that the Department restrict the importation of PV modules produced by any company owned, influenced, or controlled by a foreign adversary. Given American leadership in the renewable energy industry, we can improve security while also nurturing domestic innovation and manufacturing.Our comments below address issues related to this RFI and EO 13920 as they relate to solar PV modules. Specifically, we discuss:1. Recent Findings Support the Need for EO 139202. Understanding the PV Module-Level, System-Level, and BPS Risk3. Potential Methods for Using PV Modules to De-stabilize the BPS4. All PV Manufacturers Should Comply with U.S. Standards5. Responses to RFI Questions6. Preparing the BPS for Known UnknownsRecent Findings Support the Need for EO 13920As we will discuss below, there is a historical record of threats to the BPS from equipment compromised by foreign ownership, control or influence from adversarial nations, which remain to be remediated. The risks posed by these threats is current, serious, and well established. On August 6th the President's Working Group on Financial Markets released a report recommending that for 'Non-Cooperating Jurisdictions' such as China, "the Securities and Exchange Commission take steps to enhance the listing standards on U.S. exchanges for access to audit work papers, among other recommendations."/1First Solar believes that entities providing equipment to the BPS should be subject to comparable standards to protect the integrity of the BPS to ensure that adversarial nations do not have the opportunity to undermine America's energy security BPS.To characterize the financial and reliability risks to the BPS associated with using unregulated and compromised equipment, researchers at the Georgia Institute of Technology recently examined how high-wattage botnets would impact market functionality. Their research indicated that such tactics could create up to $350 million in economic damage to wholesale power markets in New York and California alone./2Financial manipulation of energy markets played a major role in the California Energy Crisis of 2000 and 2001, ultimately leading to long term blackouts throughout much of the state.Our comments seek to highlight potential risks arising from the supply of PV modules manufactured by companies controlled by foreign, ownership, control or influence (FOCI) of adversarial nations. Presently, more than 75% of modules entering the U.S. are imported either directly from China, or imported by companies subject to China's ownership, control, or influence. The Energy Information Administration's Annual Energy Outlook forecasts that within the next 10 years, utility scale solar PV facilities will provide 13% of the generating capacity for the electric power sector./3PV capacity in the U.S. has grown exponentially over the past decade and is projected to continue this rapid growth. The increasing use of smart communications devices within PV modules and the ability to control PV modules through remote communications as detailed in our comments below highlights a need to include PV modules within the scope of this Executive Order.Understanding the PV Module-Level, System-Level, and BPS RiskFirst Solar's leadership as an equipment provider and developer, builder and operator of solar projects currently providing electricity to the U.S. BPS has allowed us to see firsthand the risks that can arise from poor security. Our responses to the Request For Information (RFI) focus on our role as the leading U.S.-based PV module manufacturer and as an equipment vendor for power generation systems connected to the BPS. With more than 10 years of experience throughout all aspects of utility-scale solar development, construction, and operation, First Solar has unique perspective on the risks to the BPS from the unregulated deployment of potentially compromised PV modules. Such risks can be addressed with the right protocols. We strongly encourage the Department to take the following risks into account as EO 13920 is implemented.The most obvious risk to the BPS is manipulation of communications and control devices at utility-scale PV installations. As we will detail further below, First Solar is aware of the use of compromised communications and control infrastructure to overwhelm a third party's solar installation in the U.S. This action prevented the ability to control power at the site. There is a growing trend to install sophisticated communications at Bulk Power generation systems to allow for remote monitoring. For example, Dubai recently announced a partnership with a Chinese-based Engineering, Construction, and Procurement (EPC) partner for a 900 MW facility that would be operated remotely./4The remote operation will apparently be facilitated by the use of "smart PV inverters" that are able to monitor PV systems at the string level. Equipment with this type of remote operation capability can be easily manipulated to affect operation of a solar plant and thereby impact the BPS. Remote operation of Bulk Power solar generation facilities will likely become the norm in the U.S. as they represent cost savings for the owners and operators of the facilities.In addition to these well-known inverter-related risks, it is now possible for modules to be manufactured with microchips or microcontrollers that have a very small form factor (ex. 6x6mm) and can be embedded into the module's junction-box and are increasingly being used to meet the National Electric Code (NEC) 2017 standard on module level rapid shutdown. These tiny devices can leverage power generated by the module itself, while communicating out in various manners. These microchips and/or microcontrollers can be wired into the electronics of a solar module, relying on the solar module to provide power to the controller. Programming of the microcontroller can be done through a program like bootloader/5 that accepts code from a computer into the memory of the microcontroller. Given the ability to wire in the microcontroller to the module and to program the microcontroller, it is possible to manipulate operation of the plant from afar, potentially making the individual PV module a Cyber Asset, as explained below./6With the ability to manage and program the microcontroller, the solar module can also communicate data out from the plant and allow for remote control. Such communication can be accomplished through various mechanisms, including/7 from radio frequency (long range), cellular, Bluetooth, and potentially satellite. A brief review of current models being marketed that contain communications and/or control devices installed on the module reveals that at least 10 models from Chinese owned manufacturers have this capability./8These models are marketed presently for distributed energy applications, however the flexibility of PV modules is such that modules typically used for rooftop applications can also be deployed on utility-scale PV system interconnected to the BPS. Physical form factors and/or electrical wiring do not materially distinguish deployment modes for PV modules and therefore products targeting the distributed market are easily deployed in large-scale applications impacting the BPS. As imported modules can be used in a wide variety of applications that are not pre-defined in the customs process, it will be impossible to manage risks presented by modules with communication capability or even effectively monitor their importation via CBP as they look identical to a traditional PV module. We recommend that the Department restrict access to U.S. markets for any modules manufactured by entities subject to FOCI of foreign adversaries as they may contain micro-inverters, rapid shutdown devices (RSDs) or other communications or control devices that can compromise the integrity of the BPS.Our comments focus specifically on the potential risks arising from PV modules manufactured by companies owned, controlled or influenced by, adversarial nations. As discussed above, real risks arise from PV modules that can be manipulated from remote locations in a manner that would impact the BPS. Specifically, modules with communication or control technology present the possibility of a planned and coordinated attack that could compromise the stability of the BPS. It is important to note at the outset that, in addition to not being subject to FOCI from foreign adversaries, First Solar's manufacturing process globally does not use any electronics components manufactured by adversarial nations. In other words, First Solar modules mitigate security risks because they do not contain communication components at all, nor any electronics components sourced from companies under the control of adversarial nations.There is growing concern that PV modules will become subject to potential compromise by the use of a variety of communications devices that are increasingly installed directly on the module itself.As explained above, the most common occurrence today is the use of micro-inverters or rapid shutdown devices (RSDs) installed on the module that could allow a foreign adversary to remotely control individual modules, harmonize individual modules for amplified group impact and/or ultimately an entire an entire PV power plant. A growing segment of the PV market includes so-called "smart modules" with communications devices installed on the module that allow for control of the device remotely. Lastly, the possibility exists that modules imported from manufacturers subject to foreign ownership, control, or influence may include undetected electronics or communication devices that could be used for a similar purpose.Generally speaking there are 5 primary wireless communication methods that can be employed in PV modules. All of these methods pose a real risk that remote control and disruption of a PV system can surreptitiously be exercised:/9* Radio Frequency: Provides long range Sub-Ghz frequency to communicate from the receiver (Module) to the transceiver (remote). Range of signal could be upwards of 20 miles, relying on radio towers to transmit the signal to the panels.* Cellular Networks: method of increasing the number of simultaneous radio conversations that can be supported by a fixed number of radio frequency (RF) channels by limiting the range of transmitters to a single cell, to which a proportion of the available channels is allocated. NOTE: Chinese manufacturing plays a major role in wireless cellular communication./10* Wireless IP: Module is turned into a smart module by placing a micro-chip that broadcasts through wireless IP. IP communication can be compromised in many ways./11* ZigBee:/12 Low cost and low power wireless mesh solution that would allow for multiple modules to communicate with each other. This type of technology is present in many PV Tracker Plants.* Satellite: Satellite communication can be used to communicate to devices in the field. This type of communication can be levered to communicate to remote microcontrollers for transmission of data./13Given recent actions regarding security of 5G networks using equipment provided by manufacturers from adversarial nations, 5G communications capabilities provide a useful example of how these risks can be managed. Recognizing the risk from foreign adversaries, the Federal Communications Commission officially designated Huawei as a threat to U.S. communications networks as a result of its close ties to the Chinese government and military services./14Similarly, the United Kingdom moved in July to ban Huawei equipment from use in their 5G networks, citing both national security and economic concerns./155G is the next generation of wireless networks, building upon existing 4G Long-Term Evolution (LTE) infrastructure and improving the bandwidth, capacity, and reliability of wireless broadband services. 5G is intended to meet increasing data and communication requirements, including capacity for tens of billions of connected devices that will make up the Internet of Things (IoT), ultra-low latency required for critical near-real time communications, and faster speeds to support emerging technologies. 5G is expected to bring security improvements and a better user experience, but supply chain, deployment, network security, and competition and choice vulnerabilities may affect the security and resilience of 5G networks.As 5G is being built out, Chinese vendors have played a major role in providing the components of 5G Networking. This includes components of the core network infrastructure (network switches), Radio access network (Radio units), and end user equipment (mobiles phones, IOT devices). With Chinese vendors like Huawei, Xiaomi, ZTE, and OPPO are in the top five providers of such components, the following risks become amplified:1. Supply Chain: Supply Chain is susceptible to malicious or inadvertent introduction of vulnerabilities.2. Deployment: The attack surface for malicious actors has increased, due to the use of more information and communication components.3. Network Security: 5G builds upon previous generations of wireless networks, such as 4G LTE. With this legacy vulnerabilities may affect 5G equipment and networks.4. Loss of Competition: Proprietary interfaces are built by companies into their technologies. If customers are locked into a service provider or technology, they may be forced to continue with an untrusted technology or replace existing equipment.NERC CIP Low Impact provides that any site over 75MW falls under CIP Low Impact standards, which require certain cybersecurity controls to be implemented. This threshold was set in place to provide protection to the BPS. As entities are assessing their PV plants to see if they meet the threshold, they are also having to identify the site Bulk Electric System (BES) Cyber Assets.BES Cyber Asset Definition:"A Cyber Asset that if rendered unavailable, degraded, or misused would, within 15 minutes of its required operation, misoperation, or non-operation, adversely impact one or more Facilities, systems, or equipment, which, if destroyed, degraded, or otherwise rendered unavailable when needed, would affect the reliable operation of the Bulk Electric System. Redundancy of affected Facilities, systems, and equipment shall not be considered when determining adverse impact. Each BES Cyber Asset is included in one or more BES Cyber Systems."/16If a module has a micro-device installed on it, making it programmable, it in turn is considered a Cyber Asset. Given the risk that modules in a PV plant could be taken down or degraded collectively, the modules would also necessarily be defined as a BES Cyber Asset.Potential Methods for using PV Modules to De-stabilize and Installation on the BPSAs modules are being developed, they increasingly are being built with microcontrollers wired into the module. The module so configured has the ability to provide the necessary power from the solar module, while also establishing and maintaining communication to a remote transceiver. Modules equipped with communication devices can be connected together in the form of a botnet that would allow for control over all modules, which in turn would make them IOT devices. With the ability to connect to all modules at the same time, an adversarial actor could shut down the plant, or disable or overwork the modules to cause a system overload.As modules have transformed into IOT devices, they are more at risk of being subject to a Distributed Denial of Service (DDoS) attack that overloads the modules, with the resulting potential to take the modules offline. With communications and/or control established to the modules, an adversarial actor could also increase power to the point where equipment and personnel safety is a concern. As discussed above, First Solar is aware of such a DDoS overload event at the inverter level for a third party's PV Plant. The DDoS attack affected a field inverter, which in turn lost communication to the supervisory control and data acquisition (SCADA) system. This resulted in loss of control over the inverter, which had the potential to become a safety event and to jeopardize the ability of the plant to produce and regulate power. Depending on the size of a site and its location within the BPS transmission topology, the impact of such an event could reach beyond the plant and adversely affect the BPS. This is the equivalent of losing control over a car's gas pedal on a busy major highway. The impact to traffic from the loss of control of a single car may be difficult to predict but severe consequences are certainly foreseeable.PV systems often communicate to utilities, aggregators, and other grid operators over the public internet; consequently the power system attack surface has become significantly expanded. At the same time, solar energy systems are equipped with a range of grid-support functions that, if controlled or programmed improperly, present a risk of power system disturbances.1. Systems are typically configured with default credentials that are never changed.2. Typical OT-ICS systems are not patched and are volatile when scanned with vulnerability scanners.3. Typical OT or ICS systems are built with minimal hardening applied. This includes lack of security controls, protections and monitoring in place.The most vulnerable installations are those that are connected to communications systems that are not locked down. One of the more recognizable electric system compromises to date occurred in the Ukraine in 2015./17While they were considered secure in design and implementation, gaps existed within their defense-in-depth strategy. Had those gaps not existed, system operators may have been alerted earlier that malicious individuals were taking action to undermine the system. As noted by investigators of the event:"The power wasn't out long in Ukraine: just one to six hours for all the areas hit. But more than two months after the attack, the control centers are still not fully operational, according to a recent US report. Ukrainian and US computer security experts involved in the investigation say the attackers overwrote firmware on critical devices at 16 of the substations, leaving them unresponsive to any remote commands from operators. The power is on, but workers still have to control the breakers manually."Ukraine's experience points towards the need to proactively address protocols for all devices with communications capability that may feed into the Bulk Power System, including PV modules.National Electric Code (NEC) 2017 requires "Rapid Shutdown" of PV modules./18This code modification is done mainly for fire safety, to limit voltage and current between the modules and the inverters. Today most projects utilize discreet RSD devices however more module companies are integrating chips into their junction-boxes that allow for the module itself to meet RSD requirements./19While NEC 2017 is really only applicable for distributed PV applications it stands to reason that module manufacturers will standardize on these integrated RSD designs to lower costs and ensure their product can be used universally.From a grid stability standpoint solar modules can no longer be considered dumb devices. Even with secured inverters, transformers, and other grid facing technologies if solar panels with integrated RSD devices are supplying Bulk Power by their design they are intended to be controlled remotely and turned off within 30 seconds of a shutdown command. While shutting off a single solar panel may not disrupt a system, a coordinated cyberattack could disrupt thousands of modules across multiple systems would potentially cripple service territory and the broader network. By throttling an array on and off you could cause substantial damage to the transmission and distribution infrastructure. Securing the Bulk Power Supply begins with securing power generation and that starts at the module level.All PV Manufacturers Should Comply with U.S. StandardsFirst Solar offers PV modules that are safe and that cannot undermine the security of the Bulk Power System. First Solar modules do not contain communication components, and moreover, there are no components in the First Solar modules that could be remotely manipulated by a foreign adversary. As part of First Solar's unique thin-film manufacturing process we import limited raw materials from nations subject to this order. However none of those materials include or incorporate electronics or communications devices that could in any way be used to control PV modules. Any materials sourced from adversarial nations are subject to stringent quality controls and are either chemical or structural in nature with no role in controlling the operation of the module itself. First Solar modules cannot be remotely manipulated in a way that would put the Bulk Power System at risk.While most of our U.S. manufactured PV modules are used to supply U.S. projects, we do incorporate modules imported from our global facilities in U.S. projects. First Solar's global facilities are located in non-adversarial nations. It is important to note that all of our imported modules have the same inherent protections outlined above.In addition, materials used in our non-U.S. factories in Malaysia and Vietnam are subject to rigorous protections and restrictions. First Solar is a Tier 3 Participant in the Customs Trade Partnership Against Terrorism (CTPAT). CTPAT members agree to work with U.S. Customs and Border Protection to protect the supply chain, identify security gaps, and implement specific security measures and best practices. Within the CTPAT program, a broad range of security topics are addressed including physical security, personnel security, cybersecurity, and security screening of business partners. As a Tier 3 Participant, First Solar conducts various annual risk assessments that identify threats, assess risk and apply necessary measures to mitigate vulnerabilities. Even as an importer, First Solar offers a higher level of security and reliability; at a minimum, the same standards for compliance and transparency should apply to all other PV module importers.Importers who are members of CTPAT are considered to be of lower risk than non-CTPAT importers. First Solar is unaware of any module manufacturers owned, controlled, or influenced by foreign adversaries which are members of CTPAT, an important consideration as the Department considers approaches to ensure the supply of BPS equipment. Given that First Solar's module manufacturing does not occur in adversarial nations and that the company and its operations are not subject to any foreign ownership, control or influence by adversarial nations, First Solar modules are clearly not subject to this Executive Order. The superior security measures undertaken by First Solar combined with the fact that First Solar modules do not present communication risks that could undermine the Bulk Power System further emphasize the security of First Solar PV modules as reliable BPS components. By contrast it is unclear what security measures are taken by PV manufacturers owned, controlled or influenced by foreign adversaries, and whether those measures are sufficient to ensure the security of the U.S. BPS from interference by foreign adversaries. As a first step to protect against this risk, no modules should be allowed to enter the U.S. that have not been subject to the same security measures imposed by the nation's largest PV module manufacturer.We appreciate the Department's interest in the security and reliability of the BPS as it relates to components imported into the U.S. by manufacturers subject to control, ownership, or influence by foreign adversaries. As the leading U.S. manufacturer of PV modules, First Solar takes these issues seriously. We look forward to engaging with the Department as it moves toward implementation of Executive Order 13920.View full comment at: https://www.regulations.gov/contentStreamer?documentId=DOE-HQ-2020-0028-0071&attachmentNumber=1&contentType=pdfRespectfully Submitted,Julie MyerholtzChief Information Security Officer419-662-8500jmyerholtz@firstsolar.comwww.firstsolar.com* * *Footnotes:1/ https://home.treasury.gov/news/press-releases/sm10862/ https://www-wired-com.cdn.ampproject.org/c/s/www.wired.com/story/hackers-iot-botnets-manipulate-energy-markets/amp3/ EIA AEO 2020 Tables 8, 9 & 164/ https://www.pv-magazine.com/2020/07/29/remote-operation-for-900-mw-fifth-phase-of-dubai-solar-field/5/ https://www.electroschematics.com/working-bootloaders-build-bootloader-1/6/ https://www.nerc.com/files/glossary_of_terms.pdf7/ https://www.microcontrollertips.com/wireless-mcus-bluetooth-wi-fi-zigbee/8/ Jinko model numbers: JKMS320M, JKMS325M, JKMS330M, JKMS335M, JKMS340M; Trump Accuses TikTok of Spreading Coronavirus Conspiracy Theories after Claiming Virus Came from Wuhan LabNewsweek.comAugust 7, 2020 Friday 5:11 AM ESTCopyright © 2020 Newsweek Inc. All Rights ReservedLength: 846 wordsByline: Jason MurdockHighlight: "This mobile application may also be used for disinformation campaigns...such as when TikTok videos spread debunked conspiracy theories about the origins of the novel coronavirus," the order read.BodyPresident Donald Trump's executive order formalizing his threats against TikTok in the U.S. warned the app has been used to spread "debunked conspiracy theories" about COVID-19, despite his own long history in spreading mistruths about the virus.The executive order says TikTok collects huge amounts of data on users that can be provided to the Chinese Communist Party. It says this poses a threat to the U.S., potentially the country to "conduct corporate espionage.""[TikTok] may also be used for disinformation campaigns that benefit [China], such as when TikTok videos spread debunked conspiracy theories about the origins of the... novel coronavirus," the order says.Misinformation about the novel coronavirus swept across social media in recent months as COVID-19 spread worldwide, with Facebook singled out as being one major source. But there was another, and it was sitting inside the White House.In May, Trump claimed to have seen evidence the infectious disease could be traced to a lab in Wuhan, the Chinese city that first recorded an outbreak of the virus."Whether they [China] made a mistake or whether it started off as a mistake, and then they made another one or—did somebody do something on purpose?" he questioned in a White House briefing at the time, seemingly flirting with the conspiracy in circulation at the time suggesting the disease could have been a type of bioweapon.Trump was also asked about the source of the virus during a press conference the same month, questioned: "Have you seen anything at this point that gives you a high degree of confidence that the Wuhan Institute of Virology was the origin of this virus?"He replied: "Yes, I have. Yes, I have. And I think the World Health Organization should be ashamed of themselves because they're like the public relations agency for China."Mike Pompeo, U.S. secretary of state, once said there was "enormous evidence" to say COVID-19 was tied to the Wuhan Institute of Virology. The U.S. intelligence community later concluded the virus was "not man-made or genetically modified."Trump, with his fingers on social media, regularly shares false information, including a claim this week that children are "almost immune" to the COVID-19 virus, a post that forced Facebook to act after months of reluctance to sanction the president.Last week Trump retweeted a video containing lies about hydroxychloroquine treatment, a clip showing people claiming "you don't need masks" and there was already a "cure," despite advice from global health officials saying exactly the opposite.The president has repeatedly said COVID-19 will disappear on its own and previously promoted the debunked idea it was similar to the common flu, or cold.He shocked the scientific community in April by appearing to suggest that disinfectants could potentially be used to treat the novel coronavirus in patients. "I see the disinfectant, where it knocks it out in a minute. One minute. And is there a way we can do something like that, by injection inside or almost a cleaning," he said."Because you see it gets in the lungs and it does a tremendous number on the lungs. So it would be interesting to check that. So, that, you're going to have to use medical doctors with. But it sounds—it sounds interesting to me."Microsoft is currently exploring an acquisition of the TikTok app from Chinese company ByteDance, with Trump giving it 45 days to complete a business deal. He has suggested the U.S. Treasury should be given a cut of money if the purchase goes ahead.The U.S. has warned TikTok's Chinese ownership poses a national security concern, although restrictions come as the president is engaged in a trade conflict with Beijing."TikTok automatically captures vast swaths of information from users including internet and other network activity information such as location data and browsing and search histories," the executive order reads, banning U.S. transactions with the firm."This data collection threatens to allow the Chinese Communist Party access to Americans' personal and proprietary information—potentially allowing China to ... build dossiers of personal information for blackmail, and conduct corporate espionage."TikTok also reportedly censors content that the Chinese Communist Party deems politically sensitive, such as content concerning protests in Hong Kong and China's treatment of Uyghurs and other Muslim minorities. [TikTok] may also be used for disinformation campaigns that benefit [China], such as when TikTok videos spread debunked conspiracy theories about the origins of the... novel coronavirus."The White House has been contacted for comment.TikTok has repeatedly denied that it shares data with the Chinese government, saying that U.S. user data is actually stored in the states, with a backup in Singapore.In its response to the executive order, TikTok said: "We will pursue all remedies available to us in order to ensure that the rule of law is not discarded and that our company and our users are treated fairly—if not by the administration, then by the U.S. courts."Link to ImageGraphic President Donald TrumpSamuel Corum/GettyPresident Donald Trump departs the White House for a trip to Ohio where he will visit a Whirlpool factory on August 6, 2020 in Washington, DC. After the visit to the factory he will attend a fundraising reception and then head to his properties in New Jersey for the weekend.Load-Date: August 7, 2020End of Document

TikTok sues Trump administration over ban: 'We have no choice'UPIAugust 24, 2020 Monday 12:56 PM ESTCopyright 2020 U.P.I. All Rights ReservedLength: 312 wordsByline: SOMMER BROKAWDateline: Aug. 24 BodyByteDance, the parent company of TikTok, filed a lawsuit against the Trump administration Monday over efforts to ban the Chinese video service in the United States.President Donald Trump banned the social app with an executive order this month, citing national security concerns. The order said the app collects Americans' personal data that could be used by the Chinese government.Monday, TikTok replied that there is no evidence to justify the ban and said it violates the Fifth Amendment of the U.S. Constitution, which guarantees due process."The executive order seeks to ban TikTok purportedly because of the speculative possibility that the application could be manipulated by the Chinese government," the company wrote in a blog post."But, as the U.S. government is well aware, [we] have taken extraordinary measures to protect the privacy and security of TikTok's U.S. user data, including by having TikTok store such data outside of China (in the United States and Singapore) and by erecting software barriers that help ensure that TikTok stores its U.S. users data separately."The company added that it has given plenty of documentation to the U.S. government outlining its security practices that should refute any concerns.TikTok has 1,500 employees in the United States and the ban threatens 10,000 more planned jobs in California, Texas, New York, Tennessee, Florida, Michigan, Illinois and Washington State, the company said."To be clear, we far prefer constructive dialogue over litigation," the blog added."We have no choice but to take action to protect our rights, and the rights of our community and employees."TikTok also said Trump's order was a misuse of federal law because it's not based on a legitimate national emergency.Trump drew ire from China earlier this month when he threatened the ban if TikTok wasn't sold to U.S. software giant Microsoft.Load-Date: August 24, 2020End of Document

Tech battle: U.S. companies call on Trump to backtrackCE Noticias Financieras EnglishAugust 28, 2020 FridayCopyright 2020 Content Engine, LLC.All Rights ReservedCopyright 2020 CE Noticias Financieras All Rights ReservedLength: 560 wordsBody             By Mauro Albornoz       Every measure announced in relation to the US-China trade war shows that technology is the battleground. Since President Donald Trump's executive order banning US companies from negotiations with the Chinese platform WeChat has been known, a concern alarm has been set on American companies working with the Asian giant. Now they fear a negative impact and ask the first representative to backtrack. While WeChat is popularly known as the "Chinese Whatsapp" it is actually a super-application that brings social, work and financial life together on a single platform. It is used to talk, buy, send money, order a taxi, play games, share photos, among many other functions. With the restrictions of the People's Party on its internet, today this application is about the digital nexus between China and the rest of the world. Its parent company Tencent is currently valued at around $69 billion, making it the eighth largest company in the world, according to Bloomberg. This year the app reached 1.2 billion active users and is the main communication bridge for many Chinese citizens to communicate with their relatives in the midst of a pandemic.Although Donald Trump signed executive orders banning "commercial transactions" with that Chinese platform, there are still some doubts about the scope of the announcement.  The main confusion is due to whether access to WeChat by any U.S. citizen is prohibited or the blockade is to all types of negotiations with parent company Tencent. In the second case it could be due to a commercial situation already going through the Chinese company Huawei and which had a huge impact on large manufacturers in the telecommunications sector. In recent days, however, senior Trump administration officials have sought to appease the waters and reassure big business about the possibility that such a blockade may not be so restrictive. Even several technology-specialized media such as TechCrunch consider this measure to be a Trump communication strategy rather than a concrete measure that can be developed.Apple is one of the companies that could lose more with the WeChat ban because China accounts for one-fifth of its sales. It also depends on China for a large part of its manufacturing, which could be affected if the Chinese government decided to retaliate. This measure would also affect companies, from large multinationals to SMEs. The American Chamber of Commerce in Shanghai interviewed 142 representatives of US companies on Chinese soil and 88% of them agreed that this measure would have a negative impact on their operations, while 56.3% said this step would lead to their loss of competitiveness and 41.5% foreseeses negative consequences for their revenue.Tencent has a large stake in U.S. companies and made million-dollar investments in many sectors from the video game 'Fortnite' to Hollywood blockbusters. He owns Riot Games, the studio that creators two of the world's most important games: "League of Legends" and "Teamfight Tactics". In 2015, the Chinese company signed a $700 million deal with the National Basketball Association, making the company the NBA's exclusive digital partner in China.  The presidentDonald Trump seems willing to slow the advance of this tech giant even though it is not yet clear how much collateral damage this measure could trigger.Load-Date: August 29, 2020End of Document

US-Microsoft confirms its intention to buy TikTok in US after talking to TrumpCE Noticias Financieras EnglishAugust 3, 2020 MondayCopyright 2020 Content Engine, LLC.All Rights ReservedCopyright 2020 CE Noticias Financieras All Rights ReservedLength: 542 wordsBodyMADRID, 3 (EUROPA PRESS)Microsoft company has confirmed on Sunday its intention to purchase the TikTok mobile phone app in the United States after its CEO, Satya Nadella, has had a conversation with US President Donald Trump."Microsoft is ready to continue discussions to explore the purchase of TikTok in the United States," the company has reported through a statement disseminated through its 'blog'.According to the company, it will engage in talks with TikTok's owner Company, ByteDance, "quickly" with the aim of ending in "weeks". On the possible date, it has progressed that discussions may be complete "by September 15". During the process, Microsoft has assured that it "hopes to continue its dialogue with the U.S. government, including with the president."Discussions with ByteDance will be based on a notification to the U.S. Foreign Investment Committee. In the statement, the company states that both have notified their intention to explore a preliminary proposal that would involve the purchase of TikTok not only in the United States, but also in Canada, Australia and New Zealand.The new structure "would build on the experience that TikTok users currently love, while adding security, privacy and digital security protections." Thus, "the operating model for the service would be built to ensure transparency to users, as well as adequate security oversight by the governments of these countries," according to the text."Among other measures, Microsoft would ensure that all private data of U.S. users is transferred and remain in the United States," he added, while ensuring that data currently stored outside the U.S. country will be deleted after being transferred.In this regard, Microsoft has attached importance to "addressing Trump's concerns" and has pledged to acquire TikTok after "a comprehensive security review" and to provide "the right economic benefits to the United States, including the Treasury.""Microsoft appreciates the personal involvement of Trump and the U.S. government as it continues to develop strong security protections for the country," he said.Finally, the company has emphasized that, so far, discussions are in a "preliminary" phase. Thus, you have specified that you "have no intention" to "provide more updates until the discussions do not have a final result".U.S. Secretary of State Mike Pompeo confirmed on Sunday that Trump is finalizing preparations to make a decision on restricting the use of the app on suspicion that he would be sending information from his users to the Chinese government.The president already announced Friday that he would ban TikTok using emergency economic powers or an executive order, although he did not provide further details on it.Critics of the app were wary of whether the data TikTok could collect from US users could end up in the hands of the Chinese government, even though the company has assured it stores its data outside China and would resist any attempt by Beijing to seize the information.For their part, cybersecurity experts have pointed out that the potential risk to national security posed by the application is, above all, theoretical and that there is no evidence to suggest that TikTok user data may becompromised by China's intelligence.Load-Date: August 4, 2020End of Document

US tightens its restrictions by en closing Huawei's access to technology and chips around the worldCE Noticias Financieras EnglishAugust 17, 2020 MondayCopyright 2020 Content Engine, LLC.All Rights ReservedCopyright 2020 CE Noticias Financieras All Rights ReservedLength: 513 wordsBodyThe U.S. Department of Commerce has today unveiled new rules restricting the Chinese telecom equipment manufacturer's ability huawei to purchase foreign-produced semiconductors using U.S. technology.This new regulation is based on the restrictions already announced in May that prevent the Chinese company from being able to access the chips manufactured with American technology, even if huawei was designed. In statements to Fox Business, Commerce Secretary Wilbur Ross explained that these restrictions led the Chinese entity to take "some evasive measures" that allowed it to access such semiconductors through third parties. "The new regulation clarifies that any use of U.S.-made software or equipment is prohibited and requires a license," the official stressed.The Ross-led Department also added 38 other Huawei subsidiaries in 21 countries to its "List of Entities," which prevents companies from exporting U.S. technology to those entities without a license. Huawei has been blacklisted since last year along with a total of 152 of its affiliates. A new rule requires companies on this list to obtain a license when a firm such as Huawei acts "as a buyer, interim consignee, end consignee or end user".In a statement, Secretary of State Mike Pompeo explained that the Trump Administration considers Huawei "an arm of the Chinese Communist Party (CHP)" surveillance state" and therefore continues to take such measures. "We will not tolerate the PCCh's efforts to undermine the privacy of our citizens, the intellectual property of our companies, or the integrity of the next generation's networks around the world," he added. Relations between Washington and Beijing are at its worst in decades, and the US is pressuring other governments around the world to exclude Huawei from its networks, arguing that the company will hand over the data to the Chinese government for espionage. Huawei denies that it is spying for China. The new measures, which come into force immediately, should avoid Huawei's attempts to circumvent US export controls, Commerce said.This decision comes after the review of the first phase of the trade agreement was cancelled over the weekend, alluding to an agenda dispute. U.S. trade representative Robert Lighthizer, Treasury Secretary Steven Mnuchin and Chinese Deputy Prime Minister Liu He were expected to address the us's advance by teleconference last Saturday. All this at a time when the PRESIDENT of the United States signed two decrees that will prohibit the use of applications such as TikTok (ByteDance) or WeChat (Tencent) in the US. In the case of TikTok, the company could save its U.S. operations if it manages to sell them to a US company, such as Microsoft and Twitter. Over the weekend, when asked during a press conference if there were other Chinese-owned companies in particular that he was considering banning, such as Alibaba, US President Donald Trump acknowledged that he was considering retaliation. "We are seeing other things, yes, " replied the representative.commentsforum0WhatsApp  FacebookTwitter  Linkedin       Load-Date: August 18, 2020End of Document

Walmart joins Microsoft in bid for video app TikTokAssociated Press Financial WireAugust 27, 2020 Thursday 11:28 PM GMTCopyright 2020 Associated Press All Rights ReservedSection: BUSINESS NEWS; DOMESTIC NEWSLength: 461 wordsByline: JOSEPH PISANI and ZEN SOO, AP Technology WritersBodyWalmart is the latest company to want a piece of TikTok, the popular Chinese-owned video app that has come under fire from the Trump administration.The world's largest retailer made a joint bid with tech giant Microsoft to buy TikTok's U.S. business, according to a person close to the deal who isn't authorized to discuss the terms publicly.It may seem like an unlikely combination, but Microsoft and Walmart are already business partners. Microsoft provides cloud computing services that help run the retailer’s stores and online shopping. The two companies signed a 5-year partnership in 2018, enabling them to join forces against a shared rival: online shopping giant Amazon.Walmart said in a statement Thursday that a deal with Microsoft and TikTok could help it expand its advertising business and reach more shoppers.TikTok, through its fun, goofy videos, has gained 100 million U.S. users. But the White House is pushing TikTok's Chinese owner ByteDance to sell its U.S. business if it wants to keep operating in the country, citing security risks.Microsoft has confirmed discussions with TikTok; other tech companies such as Oracle are also reportedly interested in a possible acquisition. While ByteDance hasn't disclosed its asking price for TikTok, the Wall Street Journal reported Thursday that it is seeking roughly $30 billion for TikTok's U.S. operations. The Journal reported that bidders so far haven't been willing to meet that price. TikTok didn't immediately reply to an emailed request for comment.Over the past year, TikTok has tried to put distance between its app and its Chinese ownership. It installed former top Disney executive Kevin Mayer as its American CEO, but he resigned Thursday after just a few months on the job.In a letter to employees, Mayer said that his decision to leave comes after the “political environment has sharply changed.”ByteDance launched TikTok in 2017, then bought Musical.ly, a video service popular with teens in the U.S. and Europe, and combined the two. A twin service, Douyin, is available for Chinese users.U.S. authorities are concerned that TikTok would turn over user data to Chinese authorities and that it censors content that would upset China. TikTok denies that it has shared user data with the Chinese government and would not do so if asked, nor censored videos at Chinese authorities’ request. It says it's not a national security threat.The sale situation has been unusual, with President Donald Trump demanding payment for the U.S. government for its part in orchestrating a deal, a step that experts say is unprecedented. The president said on Aug. 18 that Oracle was “a great company” that “could handle” buying TikTok. He declined to state a preference between Oracle and Microsoft as buyers.Load-Date: August 27, 2020End of Document

Trump reiterates threat to ban TikTok if sale falls throughPolitico.comAugust 3, 2020 Monday 7:05 PM GMTCopyright 2020 Capitol News Company, LLC All Rights ReservedSection: POLITICOPICKSLength: 831 wordsHighlight: "I don't mind if, whether it's Microsoft or somebody else, a big company, a secure company, a very American company buy[s] it," the president said.BodyTreasury Secretary Steven Mnuchin wants a TikTok sale to Microsoft. But on Monday, Trump's trade adviser Peter Navarro took to CNN to say the potential deal makes him uncomfortable, while President Donald Trump said a portion of any TikTok sale should go to the U.S. Treasury - a move critics likened to a "public shakedown."Despite a nine-month national security investigation and weeks of talk of action against TikTok, the Trump administration has yet to settle on a coherent approach to taking on the popular video-sharing app.President Donald Trump said Monday he would force TikTok to close up shop in the United States if the Chinese-owned platform is not sold to U.S. buyers by the middle of next month."I set a date of around Sep. 15, at which point it's going to be out of business in the United States," Trump told reporters Monday at the White House, just days after he announced plans to shut the app down over security concerns as soon as this past weekend.The app has exploded in popularity during the coronavirus pandemic, and has been the most downloaded non-game app in the world this summer, according to data analytics firm SensorTower, with the U.S. accounting for the second-highest volume of downloads.But the app, owned by Chinese tech giant ByteDance, has come under fire from lawmakers and government officials on both sides of the aisle over fears that it could turn over American users' information to the Chinese government. TikTok says it has not provided consumers' personal information to Beijing and would not do so.Committee on Foreign Investment in the U.S., or CFIUS, opened an investigation in November into ByteDance's 2017 acquisition of Musical.ly, a $1 billion deal that didn't undergo foreign investment review at the time. Microsoft has emerged as the leading candidate to buy TikTok and said Sunday that the company will push forward with its discussions about a potential purchase.TikTok didn't respond to a request for comment. Microsoft declined to comment beyond the Sunday blog post.Mnuchin - whose agency leads the CFIUS process - is in favor of the deal, according to a person familiar with the acquisition talks who spoke on condition of anonymity because negotiations are ongoing. But on Monday morning, Navarro - a key architect of Trump's strategy on China - told CNN he had qualms about a deal between Microsoft and TikTok."Microsoft should divest its Chinese holdings and then we feel more comfortable," Navarro said, noting that Microsoft's Bing search engine and Skype videoconferencing platform are used by the Chinese Communist Party. "This is not a white-hat company," he added. Trump told reporters Monday afternoon he had spoken to Microsoft CEO Satya Nadella about a sale over the weekend because TikTok is "too big, too invasive" to be controlled by China. The president also said that he wanted to see a "substantial portion" of TikTok's acquisition cost "come into" the U.S. Treasury, though it is unclear what process he was referring to."We are making it possible for this deal to happen. Right now they don't have any rights, unless we give it to them. If we give them the rights, it has to come into this country," he said, likening the relationship to that of a landlord and tenant. "Without a lease, the tenant has nothing.""It's a great asset, but it's not a great asset in the United States unless they have the approval of the United States," Trump said he indicated to Nadella.Neither the White House nor Treasury would offer comments clarifying Trump's remarks. Companies pay CFIUS a filing fee when deals are reviewed, but those fees max out at $300,000. During a press briefing Monday evening, Trump again reiterated that the U.S. Treasury should get part of the proceeds from TikTok's sale because it helped broker the deal by threatening to ban it."Either way, whether it's Microsoft or somebody else, the Chinese, the United States should get a large percentage of that price because we're making it possible," he said.The president's comments were quickly panned online, with Trump critic and political campaign strategist Rick Wilson comparing it to a "public shakedown." Jesse Blumenthal, who manages the technology and innovation work at the conservative Charles Koch Institute, said he had concerns about ByteDance's ownership of TikTok but expressed shock at Trump's comments: "this is not how this works."Karen North, a professor of digital social media at USC's Annenberg School, said Trump's politicization of TikTok was unfortunate because it obscures legitimate privacy concerns."The data situation with TikTok, regardless of the politics, is actually a serious problem and one people should worry about," said North, who served in the White House Office of Science and Technology Policy during the Clinton Administration. TikTok sued Donald Trump for banning its U.S. operationsCE Noticias Financieras EnglishAugust 24, 2020 MondayCopyright 2020 Content Engine, LLC.All Rights ReservedCopyright 2020 CE Noticias Financieras All Rights ReservedLength: 465 wordsBodyIn an open legal dispute with Donald Trump's administration, social video enforcement TikTok, owned by China's ByteDance,sued the U.S. government for enacting a law prohibiting any transaction or business with that company in the U.S.In his lawsuit, TikTok alleged that President Trump's executive order, signed in early August, "is not based on genuine national security concerns,"according to excerpts from the court document the company itself posted on a blog on Monday."The executive order issued by the Administration on August 6, 2020 has the potential to strip our community of rights without any evidence to justify such extreme and due process," TikTok said in that complaint.The Trump Administration argues that TikTok poses a "threat" to U.S. national security,so it prevented its transactions from September 15, and is seeking to force its sale to a U.S. company like Microsoft, which has shown interest in the popular social network.In his executive order, Trump said TikTok creates "real risks" for the US. Usa,so he banned any business with his parent company, a move that can force ByteDance to sell his social network.The lawsuit, filed monday in the Federal District Court for the Central District of California, in Los Angeles, represents another step in the dispute between TikTok and the White House.It all started with Trump's first executive order against TikTok, which derives from his legal authority of the International Emergency Economic Powers Act, which allows the president to regulate economic transactions in a national emergency.Previous administrations have used it to sanction foreign governments, as well as drug kingpins and hackers, but have never used it against a global technology company.TikTok, which in the U.S. has more than 80 million users,is one of the most growing social networks in recent years, where it has become the main entertainment for many teens and a marketing channel for major celebrities.The Trump Administration, as well as leading Democratic congressmen, have insisted that the widespread use of TikTok puts American data privacy and national security at risk as it is exposed through ByteDance to chinese government instructions and intervention.China's Foreign Ministry also opposes the forced sale of TikTok, an operation it believes violates the principles of the World Trade Organization (WTO).Indeed, the Chinese government sees the intention to sell TikTok as another chapter of the trade war between Beijing and Washington, which seeks to contain the growing technological power of the Asian giant, which has already seen the telecommunications firm Huawei face restrictions.Attempts to put pressure on TikTok are also seen with concern by tech giants with global ambitions such as Alibaba and Tencent. Chinese tech companies could face trouble in EuropePolitico.comAugust 8, 2020 Saturday 3:17 PM GMTCopyright 2020 Capitol News Company, LLC All Rights ReservedSection: POLITICOPICKSLength: 1456 wordsHighlight: After Huawei, all eyes are on TikTok. Who's next?The United States government this week launched a new phase in its campaign to push back on Chinese technology firms, announcing efforts to block cloud and software companies from China from thriving in the West.The move was flanked by a decision Thursday to block business dealings with social media company TikTok and chat app WeChat and follows a two-year-long diplomatic campaign against telecom giant Huawei, as officials warned repeatedly that these firms don't play by Western rules, particularly when it comes to snooping and data protection.And there's more coming.U.S. Secretary of State Mike Pompeo, who is scheduled to visit Central Europe next week, signaled Washington's plans to use its trade and export tools against a broader range of Chinese technology firms. Those efforts will likely spill over into Europe, where privacy concerns, human rights woes, security and trade interests are advancing political pushback against Chinese tech.POLITICO lists the likely targets in China's technology sector that are drawing the attention of security authorities, data protection groups, trade officials and China hawks alike.TikTok is owned by Chinese tech giant ByteDance and has a sister application in China called Douyin. The app has become immensely popular with young people worldwide: It was the most downloaded app in the first three months of 2020.What's the fuss: U.S. President Donald Trump issued an executive order Thursday banning "transactions" with ByteDance, alleging the company shares user data with the Chinese state. ByteDance denies the allegations, saying its user data is stored in the U.S. and Singapore. It said it will build a new data center in Ireland. It is now mulling a sale of a chunk of its business to Microsoft to avoid the ban.Between the lines: Like most advertising-funded social media platforms, TikTok collects a lot of user data, raising privacy concerns. The platform's young clientele is an added cause for concern.What Europe thinks: Calls for a ban have so far not been echoed in Europe, even in capitals that have taken tough stances against Huawei. Europe's grouping of privacy regulators set up a task force to look into TikTok earlier this year, and several national watchdogs are also investigating the app.The new Irish data center could shake up ongoing privacy probes in Europe, which could be entrusted to the Irish Data Protection Commission.WeChat, owned by Tencent, is sometimes called a "super app," offering messaging, social media and payment functionalities to its users. It's relatively small outside China.What's the fuss: Trump issued an executive order against WeChat, similar to the one on TikTok, barring "transactions" with the company. Pompeo said earlier on Fox News that the app was "feeding data directly to the Chinese Communist Party."Between the lines: WeChat would have access to large amounts of personal data from its individual users. A recent report by the Toronto-based digital rights research organization Citizen Lab also showed the app used its international users' posts to train its domestic version to better censor the Chinese internet.What Europe thinks: Privacy and human rights concerns could play well with European digital rights groups and politicians.Alibaba is China's dominant e-commerce and cloud company. The firm has data centers in China, the U.S., Germany and the U.K., among other countries.What's the fuss: Founder Jack Ma met Trump days before he took office as president in 2017, and promised to create 1 million jobs in the U.S. Relations have soured since but Alibaba's name had remained largely absent from the U.S. onslaught against Chinese tech giants. That changed this week, when Pompeo announced plans to create "Clean Cloud," which would bar Alibaba from getting access to U.S. citizens' personal data and companies' corporate data.Another concern goes back to last year, when the city of Hangzhou said it would embed state officials at tech companies including Alibaba - blurring the lines between the company and the state.What Europe thinks: Alibaba's cloud services arm has data centers in Europe and caters to the Continent's companies, like Dutch electronics firm Philips. But Europe also wants to move away from foreign cloud providers.Alibaba has also faced harsh criticism from European consumer groups, who warn that the company's e-commerce platform floods the European market with illegal and dangerous items.Founded in 1997 by the son of former Chinese leader Hu Jintao, Nuctech is a state-owned manufacturer of X-ray machines, scanners and explosive detection systems.What's the fuss: Nuctech is owned by an industrial conglomerate that also works in China's nuclear sector, raising security concerns. Also, the EU put anti-dumping duties on the company's cargo scanning equipment a decade ago. But in recent years it has found success in selling security gear to European airports."It's extremely worrisome if a foreign state-funded company gets more and more control over our strategic infrastructure," German conservative MEP Axel Voss told POLITICO previously about Nuctech's success in Europe.Between the lines: One worry is that the gear could be used as a transmitter of malicious software. Another is that Nuctech equipment could cause disruption at Europe's airports and borders if it were to be misused by foreign actors.The case of Nuctech also poses tough questions for Europe's competition authorities, who have trouble protecting the European market from Chinese companies that undercut the prices of European players.DJI is a private drone-manufacturing company based in Shenzhen. It's the biggest beneficiary of the explosion in the world's drone market, securing a 74 percent share, according to 2018 estimates.What's the fuss: The U.S. government has moved away from using DJI drones in recent years. Security officials fear the data gathered through the drones could include detailed imagery of critical infrastructure, military hardware, frontiers or nuclear sites. DJI officials rushed to assuage concerns, insisting it has access to "essentially none" of the data its drones produce.Between the lines: The main problem is that DJI's non-Chinese alternatives are considered up to 10 times as expensive - a hard pill to swallow for public authorities procuring gear.What Europe thinks: National and local governments across the bloc are using DJI drones for surveillance and inspection purposes, as POLITICO has reported. Government authorities have largely dismissed concerns by arguing the drones are only used for non-sensitive work, but both German and French militaries as well as British police use their drones.Video surveillance company Hikvision is a market leader in providing closed-circuit television and surveillance systems to public authorities across the world. Its controlling shareholder is a state-owned defense conglomerate.What's the fuss: The U.S. in 2018 barred the company from providing equipment to the federal government. In October 2019, it also added the company to the export controls "entity list" due to its work with public authorities in Xinjiang, where the government is accused of persecuting the Uighurs, a Muslim ethnic group.What Europe thinks: The company has subsidiaries in a number of European countries. It has had success selling to British authorities, in particular, despite calls from national politicians to investigate the company.At the heart of political concerns around Chinese tech is a cocktail of trade worries, human rights woes, security interests and data protection considerations - as both the U.S. and Europe seek to rebalance diplomatic relations with Beijing.One concern China's tech companies face is the country's domestic intelligence and security legislation, which "obliges" companies to "assist and cooperate" state intelligence services. Another is that Chinese security services could more easily hack and manipulate software and hardware from domestic firms, as the EU grows "more suspicious" of Chinese cyber espionage campaigns.Governments also fear that European champions might get outcompeted by China's growing companies. And Chinese tech companies have come under scrutiny for their role in supporting the Beijing government's detention of Uighur Muslims in the Xinjiang province and the crackdown on protests in Hong Kong.Critics of the campaign against China's tech firms have pointed out these risks aren't limited to Chinese products: Many Western firms have China-based manufacturing plants and deep commercial ties with Chinese manufacturers. In Europe, critics also point to the U.S.'s own mass surveillance schemes exposed by whistleblower Edward Snowden years ago.Load-Date: August 9, 2020End of Document

TikTok Sues Trump Administration Over App BanCE Noticias Financieras EnglishAugust 25, 2020 TuesdayCopyright 2020 Content Engine, LLC.All Rights ReservedCopyright 2020 CE Noticias Financieras All Rights ReservedLength: 461 wordsBody       TikTok, a popular Chinese video-sharing mobile app that is being banned by U.S President Donald Trump, has filed a lawsuit in federal court challenging the Administration's decision. It was on August 6 that Trump signed the Executive Order to ban TikTok, along with another Chinese firm WeChat, citing a threat to national security, foreign policy, and economy of the United States. In a blog post, the company owned by China's ByteDance Ltd. said it strongly disagrees with the Administration's position that TikTok is a national security threat. According to the firm, 100 million Americans turn to TikTok at present for entertainment, inspiration, and connection, and it has more than 1,500 employees across the country. The company has also planned 10,000 more jobs in California, Texas, New York, Tennessee, Florida, Michigan, Illinois, and Washington State. Announcing the lawsuit filing, TikTok said, "The Executive Order issued by the Administration on August 6, 2020 has the potential to strip the rights of that community without any evidence to justify such an extreme action, and without any due process." In its order, the U.S. government had said its decision cites the speculative possibility that the application could be manipulated by the Chinese government. In response, TikTok in its complaint noted its extraordinary measures to protect the privacy and security of TikTok's U.S. user data. TikTok said it stores such data outside of China, on servers located in the United States and Singapore, and has erected software barriers that help ensure that TikTok stores its U.S. user data separately from the user data of other ByteDance products. Further, its key personnel, including CEO, Global Chief Security Officer, and General Counsel, are all Americans based in the United States, and therefore are not subject to Chinese law.  In its complaint, the company alleged that the Administration ignored its extensive efforts to address concerns. TikTok also said the Executive Order is a misuse of International Emergency Economic Powers Act or IEEPA as it authorizes the prohibition of activities that have not been found to be "an unusual and extraordinary threat." "By banning TikTok with no notice or opportunity to be heard (whether before or after the fact), the executive order violates the due process protections of the Fifth Amendment," the company stated. The company said it does not take suing the government lightly, but has no other choice to protect its rights, and the rights of its community and employees.   As per the order, TikTok will get 45 days before the ban comes in to effect. Meanwhile, there are reports that major U.S. tech firms Microsoft, Twitter and Oracle are in the race to buy TikTok's U.S operations.        Load-Date: August 26, 2020End of Document

And we always tell them, we don't talk about anything that happened in a classified setting.You know, former acting Director Grenell has stated that former staffers, people that worked for both sides of the aisle as far as administrations had told him, don't go to The Hill if you don't have to because they're going to misconstrue things that you said and leak it.I remember going to a CNN reporter two or three years ago during all the Russian collusion narrative and I said, you know, if you really want to be an American patriot, you might reveal who's doing the leaking. And he just grind and said, oh, I can't do that, and walked away.You know, we're the committee that's supposed to be seen and never heard from. And that goes throughout Congress if you're in a classified setting. And what has been taking place over the last few years is really despicable and it's really made a shambles out of the Intelligence Committee in Congress and it's got to stop.And I think that the director made a good decision. They can't say that they're not being informed. But they can't just run out to the swath of reporters that are sitting outside of briefings and go ahead and reveal information that they shouldn't be talking about.MCDOWELL: Lee Carter, jump in here.LEE CARTER, POLLSTER AND PRESIDENT, MASLANSY AND PARTNERS: Well, I -- the thing that I'm really curious about is how do you navigate the -- I mean, obviously, putting things in writing is going to be very, very important. But everything that we talk about, everything that's said is misconstrued and we -- how do you define your own narrative for yourself when it's constantly being twisted?WENSTRUP: Well, it's very difficult because at the same time that someone is doing that, twisting the narrative and going out and making it public, we're respecting what -- we're respecting the oath that we took, a signed oath that we took, as a matter of fact. So it does make it challenging. And I think that it becomes very important for Director Ratcliffe to take an action like this and let the country know why he is taking it because the American people need to know that, one, not only are these people that aren't -- that these are people that aren't adhering to their oath, but they are misrepresenting an people need to be aware of that misrepresentation and keep that in mind when they hear certain things that are coming out through leaks.MCDOWELL: The stimulus stalemate, let's talk about that, Congressman. We should be addressing the needs of schools as they reopen.
